7.6
Exponentials of categories
We now want to show that the category Cat of (small) categories and functors
is cartesian closed, by showing that any two categories C, D have an exponential
DC. Of course, we take DC = Fun(C, D), the category of functors and natural
transformations, for which we need to prove the required universal mapping
property (UMP).
Proposition 7.13. Cat is cartesian closed, with the exponentials
DC = Fun(C, D).
Before giving the proof, let us note the following. Since exponentials are unique
up to isomorphism, this gives us a way to verify that we have found the ‚Äúright‚Äù
deÔ¨Ånition of a morphism of functors. For the notion of a natural transformation
is completely determined by the requirement that it makes the set Hom(C, D)
into an exponential category. This is an example of how category theory can
serve as a conceptual tool for discovering new concepts. Before giving the proof,
we need the following.

Example 7.21. What happens if we take the functor category of two groups G

HG

would be an element h ‚ààH such that for every x ‚ààG, we have

œë : f ‚Üíg has an inverse œë‚àí1 : g ‚Üíf (conjugation by h‚àí1). But HG is still not

G ‚ÜíH, so the functor category HG has more than one object.
This suggests enlarging the category of groups to include also categories with
more than one object, but still having inverses for all arrows. Such categories
are called groupoids, and have been studied by topologists (they occur as the
collection of paths between diÔ¨Äerent points in a topological space). A groupoid
can thus be regarded as a generalized group, in which the domains and codomains

of elements x and y must match up, as in any category, for the multiplication
x ¬∑ y to be deÔ¨Åned.
It is clear that if G and H are any groupoids, then the functor category HG
is also a groupoid. Thus, we have the following proposition, the detailed proof
of which is left as an exercise.
Proposition 7.22. The category Grpd of groupoids is cartesian closed and the
inclusion functor

preserves the CCC structure.
7.8
Monoidal categories
As a further application of natural transformations, we can Ô¨Ånally give the gene-
ral notion of a monoidal category, as opposed to the special case of a strict one.
Recall from Section 4.1 that a strict monoidal category is by deÔ¨Ånition a monoid
in Cat, that is, a category C equipped with an associative multiplication functor,
‚äó: C √ó C ‚àí‚ÜíC
and a distinguished object I that acts as a unit for ‚äó. A monoidal category with
a discrete category C is just a monoid in the usual sense, and every set X gives
rise to one of these, with C the set of endomorphisms End(X) under composition.
Another example, not discrete, is now had by considering the category End(D)
of endofunctors of an arbitrary category D, with their natural transformations
as arrows; that is, let,
C = End(D),
G ‚äóF = G ‚ó¶F,
I = 1D.
This can also be seen to be a strict monoidal category. Indeed, the multiplication
is clearly associative and has 1D as unit, so we just need to check that composition
is a bifunctor End(D) √ó End(D) ‚àí‚ÜíEnd(D). Of course, for this we can use the
bifunctor lemma. Fixing F and taking any natural transformation Œ± : G ‚ÜíG‚Ä≤,

In this precise sense, a monoidal category is thus a category that is strict
monoidal ‚Äúup to natural isomorphism‚Äù‚Äîwhere the natural isomorphisms are

projections. We leave the veriÔ¨Åcation as an exercise. The reader familiar with ten-

that these, too, give examples of monoidal categories.
A further example comes from an unexpected source: linear logic. The logical
operations of linear conjunction and disjunction, sometimes written P ‚äóQ and
P ‚äïQ, can be modeled in a monoidal category, usually with extra structure œÉAB :

Here, too, we leave the veriÔ¨Åcation to the reader familiar with this logical system.
The basic theorem regarding monoidal categories is Mac Lane‚Äôs coherence
theorem, which says that ‚Äúall diagrams commute.‚Äù Somewhat more precisely, it

NATURALITY

says that any diagram in a monoidal category constructed, like those above, just
from identities, the functor ‚äó, and the maps Œ±, Œª, œÅ will necessarily commute. We
shall not state the theorem more precisely than this, nor will we give its some-
what technical proof which, surprisingly, uses ideas from proof theory related
to Gentzen‚Äôs cut elimination theorem! The details can be found in Mac Lane‚Äôs
book, Categories Work.

DeÔ¨Ånition 7.25. An equivalence of categories consists of a pair of functors

Observe that equivalence of categories is a generalization of isomorphism.

Experience has shown that the mathematically signiÔ¨Åcant properties of
objects are those that are invariant under isomorphisms, and in category theory,
identity of objects is a much less important relation than isomorphism. So it is
really equivalence of categories that is the more important notion of ‚Äúsimilarity‚Äù
for categories.
In the foregoing example SetsÔ¨Ån ‚âÉOrdÔ¨Ån, we see that every set is isomorphic
to an ordinal, and the maps between ordinals are just the maps between them
as sets. Thus, we have
1. for every set A, there is some ordinal n with A ‚àº= i(n),

where i : OrdÔ¨Ån ‚ÜíSetsÔ¨Ån is the inclusion functor.

(Ai)i‚ààI

The equivalent description of SetsI as Sets/I leads to the idea that, for a

usually does not make sense. This provides a precise notion of an ‚ÄúX-indexed

type theory there are ‚Äúdependent types‚Äù x : X ‚ä¢A(x), thought of as families of
types indexed over a type. These can be modeled as objects [[A]] ‚Üí[[X]] in the

Example 7.30. Stone duality
Many examples of equivalences of categories are given by what are called ‚Äúdua-
lities.‚Äù Often, classical duality theorems are not of the form C ‚àº= Dop (much

(or ‚Äúdual‚Äù) category of D. This is because the duality is established by a con-
struction that returns the original thing only up to isomorphism, not ‚Äúon the
nose.‚Äù Here is a simple example, which is a very special case of the far-reaching
Stone duality theorem.

8
CATEGORIES OF DIAGRAMS
In this chapter, we prove a very useful technical result called the Yoneda lemma,
and then employ it in the study of the important categories of set-valued functors
or ‚Äúdiagrams.‚Äù The Yoneda lemma is perhaps the single most used result in
category theory. It can be seen as a straightforward generalization of some simple
facts about monoids and posets, yet it has much more far-reaching applications.
8.1
Set-valued functor categories
We are going to focus on special functor categories of the form
SetsC
where the category C is locally small. Thus, the objects are set-valued functors,
F, G : C ‚ÜíSets
(sometimes called ‚Äúdiagrams on C‚Äù), and the arrows are natural transformations
Œ±, Œ≤ : F ‚ÜíG.
Where C = P, a poset, we have already considered such functors as ‚Äúvariable

8.2
The Yoneda embedding
Among the objects of SetsC are certain very special ones, namely the (covariant)
representable functors,
HomC(C, ‚àí) : C ‚ÜíSets.
Observe that for each h : C ‚ÜíD in C, we have a natural transformation
HomC(h, ‚àí) : HomC(D, ‚àí) ‚ÜíHomC(C, ‚àí)
(note the direction!) where the component at X is deÔ¨Åned by precomposition:
(f : D ‚ÜíX) ‚Üí(f ‚ó¶h : C ‚ÜíX).
Thus, we have a contravariant functor
k : Cop ‚ÜíSetsC
deÔ¨Åned by k(C) = HomC(C, ‚àí). Of course, this functor k is just the exponential
transpose of the bifunctor
HomC : Cop √ó C ‚ÜíSets
which was shown as an exercise to be functorial.
If we instead transpose HomC with respect to its other argument, we get a
covariant functor,
y : C ‚ÜíSetsCop
from C to a category of contravariant set-valued functors, sometimes called
‚Äúpresheaves.‚Äù (Or, what amounts to the same thing, we can put D = Cop and
apply the previous considerations to D in place of C.) More formally:
DeÔ¨Ånition 8.1. The Yoneda embedding is the functor y : C ‚ÜíSetsCop taking
C ‚ààC to the contravariant representable functor,
yC = HomC(‚àí, C) : Cop ‚ÜíSets
and taking f : C ‚ÜíD to the natural transformation,
yf = HomC(‚àí, f) : HomC(‚àí, C) ‚ÜíHomC(‚àí, D).
A functor F : C ‚ÜíD is called an embedding if it is full, faithful, and injective
on objects. We soon show that y really is an embedding; this is a corollary of
the Yoneda lemma.
One should thus think of the Yoneda embedding y as a ‚Äúrepresentation‚Äù of C
in a category of set-valued functors and natural transformations on some index
category. Compared to the Cayley representation considered in Section 1.5, this
has the virtue of being full: any map œë : yC ‚ÜíyD in SetsCop comes from

188
CATEGORY THEORY
a unique map h : C ‚ÜíD in C as yh = œë. Indeed, recall that the Cayley
representation of a group G was an injective group homomorphism
G ‚Ü£Aut(|G|) ‚äÜ|G||G|
where each g ‚ààG is represented as an automorphism Àúg of the set |G| of elements
(i.e., a ‚Äúpermutation‚Äù), by letting it ‚Äúact on the left,‚Äù
g(x) = g ¬∑ x
and the group multiplication is represented by composition of permutations,

g ¬∑ h = Àúg ‚ó¶Àúh.
We also showed a generalization of this representation to arbitrary categories.
Thus for any monoid M, there is an analogous representation
M ‚Ü£End(|M|) ‚äÜ|M||M|
by left action, representing the elements of M as endomorphisms of |M|.
Similarly, any poset P can be represented as a poset of subsets and inclusions
by considering the poset Low(P) of ‚Äúlower sets‚Äù A ‚äÜP, that is, subsets that are
‚Äúclosed down‚Äù in the sense that a‚Ä≤ ‚â§a ‚ààA implies a‚Ä≤ ‚ààA, ordered by inclusion.
Taking the ‚Äúprincipal lower set‚Äù
‚Üì(p) = {q ‚ààP | q ‚â§p}
of each element p ‚ààP determines a monotone injection
‚Üì: P ‚Ü£Low(P) ‚äÜP(|P|)
such that p ‚â§q iÔ¨Ä‚Üì(p) ‚äÜ‚Üì(q).
The representation given by the Yoneda embedding is closely related to these,
but ‚Äúbetter‚Äù in that it cuts down the arrows in the codomain category to just
those in the image of the representation functor y : C ‚ÜíSetsCop (since y is
full). Indeed, there may be many automorphisms Œ± : G ‚ÜíG of a group G that
are not left actions by an element, but if we require Œ± to commute with all right
actions Œ±(x ¬∑ g) = Œ±(x) ¬∑ g, then Œ± must itself be a left action. This is what the
Yoneda embedding does in general; it adds enough ‚Äústructure‚Äù to the objects yA
in the image of the representation that the only ‚Äúhomomorphisms‚Äù œë : yA ‚ÜíyB
between those objects are the representable ones œë = yh for some h : A ‚ÜíB. In
this sense, the Yoneda embedding y represents the objects and arrows of C as
certain ‚Äústructured sets‚Äù and (all of ) their ‚Äúhomomorphisms.‚Äù

Remark 8.4. Note the following:
‚Ä¢ If C is small, then SetsCop is locally small, and so Hom(yC, P) in SetsCop
is a set.
‚Ä¢ If C is locally small, then SetsCop need not be locally small. In this case,
the Yoneda lemma tells us that Hom(yC, P) is always a set.
‚Ä¢ If C is not locally small, then y : C ‚ÜíSetsCop will not even be deÔ¨Åned, so
the Yoneda lemma does not apply.
Finally, observe that the Yoneda embedding y : C ‚ÜíSetsCop is also injective
on objects. For, given objects A, B in C, if yA = yB then 1C ‚ààHom(C, C) =
yC(C) = yD(C) = Hom(C, D) implies C = D.

8.4
Applications of the Yoneda lemma
One frequent sort of application of the Yoneda lemma is of the following form:
given objects A, B in a category C, to show that A ‚àº= B it suÔ¨Éces to show
that yA ‚àº= yB in SetsCop. This ‚ÄúYoneda principle‚Äù results from the foregoing
theorem and the fact that, if F : C ‚ÜíD is any full and faithful functor, then
FA ‚àº= FB clearly implies A ‚àº= B. We record this as the following.
Corollary 8.5 (Yoneda principle). Given objects A and B in any locally
small category C,
yA ‚àº= yB
implies
A ‚àº= B.
A typical such case is this. In any cartesian closed category C, we know there
is always an isomorphism,
(AB)C ‚àº= A(B√óC),
for any objects A, B, C. But recall how involved it was to prove this directly,
using the compound universal mapping property (or a lengthy calculation in
Œª-calculus). Now, however, by the Yoneda principle, we just need to show that
y((AB)C) ‚àº= y(A(B√óC)).
To that end, take any object X ‚ààC; then we have isomorphisms:
Hom(X, (AB)C) ‚àº= Hom(X √ó C, AB)
‚àº= Hom((X √ó C) √ó B, A)
‚àº= Hom(X √ó (B √ó C), A)
‚àº= Hom(X, A(B√óC)).
Of course, it must be checked that these isomorphisms are natural in X, but that
is straightforward. For instance, for the Ô¨Årst one suppose we have f : X‚Ä≤ ‚ÜíX.
Then, the naturality of the Ô¨Årst isomorphism means that for any g : X ‚Üí(AB)C,
we have
g ‚ó¶f = g ‚ó¶(f √ó 1),
which is clearly true by the uniqueness of transposition (the reader should draw
the diagram).
Here is another sample application of the Yoneda principle.
Proposition 8.6. If the cartesian closed category C has coproducts, then C is
‚Äúdistributive,‚Äù that is, there is always a canonical isomorphism,
(A √ó B) + (A √ó C) ‚àº= A √ó (B + C).

194
CATEGORY THEORY
Proof. As in the previous proposition, we check that
Hom(A √ó (B + C), X) ‚àº= Hom(B + C, XA)
‚àº= Hom(B, XA) √ó Hom(C, XA)
‚àº= Hom(A √ó B, X) √ó Hom(A √ó C, X)
‚àº= Hom((A √ó B) + (A √ó C), X).
Finally, as in the foregoing example, one sees easily that these isos are all natural
in X.
We have already used a simple logical version of the Yoneda lemma several
times: to show that in the propositional calculus one has œï ‚ä£‚ä¢œà for some
formulas œï, œà, it suÔ¨Éces to show that for any formula œë, one has œë ‚ä¢œï iÔ¨Ä
œë ‚ä¢œà.
More generally, given any objects A, B in a locally small category C, to Ô¨Ånd
an arrow h : A ‚ÜíB it suÔ¨Éces to give one œë : yA ‚ÜíyB in SetsCop, for then
there is a unique h with œë = yh. Why should it be easier to give an arrow
yA ‚ÜíyB than one A ‚ÜíB? The key diÔ¨Äerence is that in general SetsCop
has much more structure to work with than does C; as we see, it is complete,
cocomplete, cartesian closed, and more. So one can use various ‚Äúhigher-order‚Äù
tools, from limits to Œª-calculus; and if the result is an arrow of the form yA ‚ÜíyB,
then it comes from a unique one A ‚ÜíB, despite the fact that C itself may
not admit the ‚Äúhigher-order‚Äù constructions. In that sense, the category SetsCop
is like an extension of C by ‚Äúideal elements‚Äù that permit calculations which
cannot be done in C. This is something like passing to the complex numbers
to solve equations in the reals, or adding higher types to an elementary logical
theory.

for any small category J and functor F : J ‚ÜíE, there is a limit L = lim

E √ó J ‚ÜíE.

preserves all limits.

8.6
Colimits in categories of diagrams
The notion of cocompleteness is of course the dual of completeness: a cate-
gory is cocomplete if it has all (small) colimits. Like the foregoing proposition
about the completeness of SetsCop, its cocompleteness actually follows simply
from the fact that Sets is cocomplete. We leave the proof of the following as an
exercise.

Theorem 8.14. For any small category C, the category of diagrams SetsCop is
cartesian closed. Moreover, the Yoneda embedding
y : C ‚ÜíSetsCop

form SetsCop.
8.8
Topoi
Since we are now so close to it, we might as well introduce the important notion of
a ‚Äútopos‚Äù‚Äîeven though this is not the place to develop that theory, as appealing
as it is. First we require the following generalization of characteristic functions
of subsets.
DeÔ¨Ånition 8.16. Let E be a category with all Ô¨Ånite limits. A subobject classiÔ¨Åer
in E consists of an object Œ© together with an arrow t : 1 ‚ÜíŒ© that is a ‚Äúuniversal
subobject,‚Äù in the following sense:
Given any object E and any subobject U ‚Ü£E, there is a unique arrow
u : E ‚ÜíŒ© making the following diagram a pullback:
U
- 1
E
?
?
u
- Œ©
t
?
(8.9)
The arrow u is called the classifying arrow of the subobject U ‚Ü£E; it can
be thought of as taking exactly the part of E that is U to the ‚Äúpoint‚Äù t of Œ©.
The most familiar example of a subobject classiÔ¨Åer is of course the set 2 = {0, 1}
with a selected element as t : 1 ‚Üí2. The fact that every subset U ‚äÜS of any set
S has a unique characteristic function u : S ‚Üí2 is then exactly the subobject
classiÔ¨Åer condition.
It is easy to show that a subobject classiÔ¨Åer is unique up to isomorphism: the
pullback condition is clearly equivalent to requiring the contravariant subobject
functor,
SubE(‚àí) : Eop ‚ÜíSets

202
CATEGORY THEORY
(which acts by pullback) to be representable,
SubE(‚àí) ‚àº= HomE(‚àí, Œ©).
The required isomorphism is just the pullback condition stated in the deÔ¨Ånition
of a subobject classiÔ¨Åer. Now apply the Yoneda principle, corollary 8.5, for two
subobject classiÔ¨Åers Œ© and Œ©‚Ä≤.
DeÔ¨Ånition 8.17. A topos is a category E such that
1. E has all Ô¨Ånite limits,
2. E has a subobject classiÔ¨Åer,
3. E has all exponentials.
This compact deÔ¨Ånition proves to be amazingly rich in consequences: it can
be shown for instance that topoi also have all Ô¨Ånite colimits, and that every slice
category of a topos is again a topos. We refer the reader to the books by Mac Lane
and Moerdijk (1992), Johnstone (2002), and McLarty (1995) for information on
topoi, and here just give an example (albeit one that covers a very large number
of cases).
Proposition 8.18. For any small category C, the category of diagrams SetsCop
is a topos.
Proof. Since we already know that SetsCop has all limits, and we know that
it has exponentials by Section 8.7, we just need to Ô¨Ånd a subobject classiÔ¨Åer.
To that end, we deÔ¨Åne a sieve on an object C of C to be a set S of arrows
f : ¬∑ ‚ÜíC (with arbitrary domain) that is closed under precomposition; that is,
if f : D ‚ÜíC is in S then so is f ‚ó¶g : E ‚ÜíD ‚ÜíC for every g : E ‚ÜíD (think
of a sieve as a common generalization of a ‚Äúlower set‚Äù in a poset and an ‚Äúideal‚Äù
in a ring). Then let
Œ©(C) = {S ‚äÜC1 | S is a sieve on C}
and given h : D ‚ÜíC, let
h‚àó: Œ©(C) ‚ÜíŒ©(D)
be deÔ¨Åned by
h‚àó(S) = {g : ¬∑ ‚ÜíD | h ‚ó¶g ‚ààS}.
This clearly deÔ¨Ånes a presheaf Œ© : Cop ‚ÜíSets, with a distinguished point,
t : 1 ‚ÜíŒ©
namely, at each C, the ‚Äútotal sieve‚Äù
tC = {f : ¬∑ ‚ÜíC}.

203
We claim that t : 1 ‚ÜíŒ© so deÔ¨Åned is a subobject classiÔ¨Åer for SetsCop. Indeed,
given any object E and subobject U ‚Ü£E, deÔ¨Åne u : E ‚ÜíŒ© at any object
C ‚ààC by
uC(e) = {f : D ‚ÜíC | f ‚àó(e) ‚ààU(D) ‚Ü£E(D)}
for any e ‚ààE(C). That is, uC(e) is the sieve of arrows into C that take e ‚ààE(C)
back into the subobject U.
The notion of a topos Ô¨Årst arose in the Grothendieck school of algebraic
geometry as a generalization of that of a topological space. But one of the most
fascinating aspects of topoi is their relation to logic. In virtue of the association
of subobjects U ‚Ü£E with arrows u : E ‚ÜíŒ©, the subobject classiÔ¨Åer Œ© can
be regarded as an object of ‚Äúpropositions‚Äù or ‚Äútruth-values,‚Äù with t = true.
An arrow œï : E ‚ÜíŒ© is then a ‚Äúpropositional function‚Äù of which Uœï ‚Ü£E
is the ‚Äúextension.‚Äù For, by the pullback condition (8.9), a generalized element
x : X ‚ÜíE is ‚Äúin‚Äù Uœï (i.e., factors through Uœï ‚Ü£E) just if œïx = true,
x ‚ààE Uœï
iÔ¨Ä
œïx = true
so that, again in the notation of Section 5.1,
Uœï = {x ‚ààE | œïx = true}.
This permits an interpretation of Ô¨Årst-order logic in any topos, since topoi also
have a way of modeling the logical quantiÔ¨Åers ‚àÉand ‚àÄas adjoints to pullbacks
(as described in Section 9.5).
Since topoi are also cartesian closed, they have an internal type theory des-
cribed by the Œª-calculus (see Section 6.6). Combining this with the Ô¨Årst-order
logic and subobject classiÔ¨Åer Œ© provides a natural interpretation of higher-order
logic, employing the exponential Œ©E as a ‚Äúpower object‚Äù P(E) of subobjects of
E. This logical aspect of topoi is also treated in the books already mentioned.

9
ADJOINTS
This chapter represents the high point of this book, the goal toward which we
have been working steadily. The notion of adjoint functor, Ô¨Årst discovered by
D. Kan in the 1950s, applies everything that we have learned up to now to
unify and subsume all of the diÔ¨Äerent universal mapping properties that we
have encountered, from free groups to limits to exponentials. But more import-
antly, it also captures an important mathematical phenomenon that is invisible
without the lens of category theory. Indeed, I will make the admittedly provoca-
tive claim that adjointness is a concept of fundamental logical and mathematical
importance that is not captured elsewhere in mathematics.
Many of the most striking applications of category theory involve adjoints,
and many important and fundamental mathematical notions are instances of
adjoint functors. As such, they share the common behavior and formal properties
of all adjoints, and in many cases this fact alone accounts for all of their essential
features.
9.1
Preliminary deÔ¨Ånition
We begin by recalling the universal mapping property (UMP) of free monoids:
every monoid M has an underlying set U(M), and every set X has a free monoid
F(X), and there is a function

the following diagram:

9.5
QuantiÔ¨Åers as adjoints
Traditionally, the main obstacle to the further development of algebraic logic
has been the treatment of the quantiÔ¨Åers. Categorical logic solves this problem
beautifully with the recognition (due to F.W. Lawvere in the 1960s) that they,
too, are adjoint functors.
Let L be a Ô¨Årst-order language. For any list ¬Øx = x1, . . . , xn of distinct

9.6
RAPL
In addition to the conceptual uniÔ¨Åcation achieved by recognizing constructions
as diÔ¨Äerent as existential quantiÔ¨Åers and free groups as instances of adjoints,
there is the practical beneÔ¨Åt that one then knows that these operations behave
in certain ways that are common to all adjoints. We next consider one of the
fundamental properties of adjoints: preservation of limits.
In Section 9.5, we had a string of three adjoints,
‚àÉ‚ä£‚àó‚ä£‚àÄ
and it is easy to Ô¨Ånd other such strings. For example, there is a string of four
adjoints between Cat and Sets,
V ‚ä£F ‚ä£U ‚ä£R
where U : Cat ‚ÜíSets is the forgetful functor to the set of objects
U(C) = C0.
An obvious question in this kind of situation is ‚Äúare there more?‚Äù That is, given a
functor does it have an adjoint? A useful necessary condition which shows that,
for example, the strings above stop is the following proposition, which is also
important in its own right.
Proposition 9.14. Right adjoints preserve limits (RAPL!), and left adjoints
preserve colimits.
Proof. Here is the easy way: suppose we have an adjunction

And, as above, ‚àÄx cannot be a left adjoint, since it does not have this

The proposition gives an extremely important and useful property of adjoints.
As in the foregoing examples, it can be used to show that a given functor does
not have an adjoint by showing that it does not preserve (co)limits. But also,
to show that a given functor does preserve all (co)limits, sometimes the easiest
way to proceed is to show that it has an adjoint. For example, it is very easy to
recognize that the forgetful functor U : Pos ‚ÜíSets from posets to sets has a
left adjoint (what is it?). Thus, we know that limits of posets are limits of the
underlying sets (suitably ordered). Dually, you may have shown ‚Äúby hand‚Äù as an
exercise that the coproduct of free monoids is the free monoid on the coproduct
of their generating sets
F(A) + F(B) ‚àº= F(A + B).
This now follows simply from the free ‚ä£forgetful adjunction.

The following deÔ¨Ånition captures the notion of a category having this sort
of adjoint structure. In such a category E, the slice categories can be regarded
as categories of abstract-indexed families of objects of E, and the reindexing of
such families can be carried out, with associated adjoint operations of sum and
product.

DeÔ¨Ånition 9.19. A category E is called locally cartesian closed if E has a
terminal object and for every arrow f : A ‚ÜíB in E, the composition functor
Œ£f : E/A ‚ÜíE/B
has a right adjoint f ‚àówhich, in turn, has a right adjoint Œ†f:
Œ£f ‚ä£f ‚àó‚ä£Œ†f
The choice of name for such categories is explained by the following
important fact.
Proposition 9.20. For any category E with a terminal object, the following are
equivalent:
1. E is locally cartesian closed.
2. Every slice category E/A of E is cartesian closed.
Proof. Let E be locally cartesian closed. Since E has a terminal object, products
and exponentials in E can be built as
A √ó B = Œ£BB‚àóA
BA = Œ†BB‚àóA.
Therefore, E is cartesian closed. But clearly every slice category E/X is also
locally cartesian closed, since ‚Äúa slice of a slice is a slice.‚Äù Thus, every slice of E
is cartesian closed.
Conversely, suppose every slice of E is cartesian closed. Then E has pullbacks,
since these are just binary products in a slice. Thus, we just need to construct
the ‚Äúrelative product‚Äù functor Œ†f : E/A ‚ÜíE/B along a map f : A ‚ÜíB. First,
change notation:

Combining the foregoing with the fact (theorem 8.14) that categories of
presheaves are always cartesian closed now yields the promised:
Corollary 9.24. For any small category C, the category SetsCop of presheaves
on C is locally cartesian closed.
Remark 9.25. Part of the interest in locally cartesian closed categories derives

9.8
Adjoint functor theorem
The question we now want to consider systematically is, when does a functor have
an adjoint? Consider Ô¨Årst the question, when does a functor of the form C ‚Üí
Sets have a left adjoint? If U : C ‚ÜíSets has F ‚ä£U, then U is representable
U ‚àº= Hom(F1, ‚àí), since U(C) ‚àº= Hom(1, UC) ‚àº= Hom(F1, C).
A related condition that makes sense for categories other than Sets is pre-
servation of limits. Suppose that C is complete and U : C ‚ÜíX preserves limits;
then we can ask whether U has a left adjoint. The adjoint functor theorem (AFT)
gives a necessary and suÔ¨Écient condition for this case.

Remark 9.31. 1. The theorem simply does not apply if C is not complete. In that
case, a given functor may have an adjoint, but the AFT will not tell us that.
2. It is essential that the solution set in the theorem be a set (and that C have
all set-sized limits).
3. On the other hand, if C is itself small and complete, then we can plainly
drop the solution set condition entirely. In that case, we have the following.
Corollary 9.32. If C is a small and complete category and U : C ‚ÜíX is a
functor that preserves all limits, then U has a left adjoint.

As a further consequence of the AFT, we have the following characterization
of representable functors on small complete categories.
Corollary 9.34. If C is a small and complete category, then for any functor
U : C ‚ÜíSets the following are equivalent:
1. U preserves all limits.
2. U has a left adjoint.
3. U is representable.

Remark 9.36. An important special case of the AFT that often occurs ‚Äúin
nature‚Äù is that in which the domain category satisÔ¨Åes certain conditions that
eliminate the need for the (rather unpleasant!) solution set condition entirely.
SpeciÔ¨Åcally, let A be a locally small, complete category satisfying the following
conditions:
1. A is well powered: each object A has at most a set of subobjects S ‚Ü£A.
2. A has a cogenerating set: there is a set of objects {Ai | i ‚ààI} (I some
index set), such that for any A, X and x Ã∏= y : X ‚áíA in A, there is some
s : A ‚ÜíAi (for some i) that ‚Äúseparates‚Äù x and y, in the sense that sx Ã∏= sy.
Then any functor U : A ‚ÜíX that preserves limits necessarily has a left adjoint.
In this form (also originally proved by Freyd), the theorem is usually known as
the special adjoint functor theorem (‚ÄúSAFT‚Äù). We refer to Mac Lane, V.8 for
the proof, and some sample applications.
Example 9.37. An important application of the AFT is that any equational
theory T gives rise to a free ‚ä£forgetful adjunction between Sets and the cate-
gory of models of the theory, or ‚ÄúT-algebras.‚Äù In somewhat more detail, let T
be a (Ô¨Ånitary) equational theory, consisting of Ô¨Ånitely many operation symbols,
each of some Ô¨Ånite arity (including nullary operations, i.e., constant symbols),
and a set of equations between terms built from these operations and variables.
For instance, the theory of groups has a constant u (the group unit), a unary
operation g‚àí1 (the inverse), and a binary operation g ¬∑ h (the group product),

244
CATEGORY THEORY
and a handful of equations such as g ¬∑ u = g. The theory of rings has a further
binary operation and some more equations. The theory of Ô¨Åelds is not equatio-
nal, however, because the condition x Ã∏= 0 is required for an element x to have
a multiplicative inverse. A T-algebra is a set equipped with operations (of the
proper arities) corresponding to the operation symbols in T, and satisfying the
equations of T. A homomorphism of T-algebras h : A ‚ÜíB is a function on
the underlying sets that preserves all the operations, in the usual sense. Let T-
Alg be the category of all such algebras and their homomorphisms. There is an
evident forgetful functor
U : T-Alg ‚ÜíSets.
The AFT implies that this functor always has a left adjoint F, the ‚Äúfree algebra‚Äù
functor.
Proposition 9.38. For any equational theory T, the forgetful functor from T-
algebras to Sets has a left adjoint.
Rather than proving this general proposition (for which see Mac Lane,
chapter V), it is more illuminating to do a simple example.

consider the lower sets in a poset.)

10
MONADS AND ALGEBRAS
In Chapter 9, the adjoint functor theorem was seen to imply that the category
of algebras for an equational theory T always has a ‚Äúfree T-algebra‚Äù functor, left
adjoint to the forgetful functor into Sets. This adjunction describes the notion
of a T-algebra in a way that is independent of the speciÔ¨Åc syntactic description
given by the theory T, the operations and equations of which are rather like a
particular presentation of that notion. In a certain sense that we are about to
make precise, it turns out that every adjunction describes, in a ‚Äúsyntax invariant‚Äù
way, a notion of an ‚Äúalgebra‚Äù for an abstract ‚Äúequational theory.‚Äù
Toward this end, we begin with yet a third characterization of adjunctions.
This one has the virtue of being entirely equational.

The triangle identities have the virtue of being entirely ‚Äúalgebraic‚Äù‚Äîno quanti-
Ô¨Åers, limits, Hom-sets, inÔ¨Ånite conditions, etc. Thus, anything deÔ¨Åned by adjoints

This is not only a matter of conceptual simpliÔ¨Åcation; it also has important
consequences for the existence and properties of the structures that are so
determined.

As we see in these examples, monads can, and often do, arise without
coming from evident adjunctions. In fact, the notion of a monad originally
did occur independently of adjunctions! Monads were originally also known
by the names ‚Äútriples‚Äù and sometimes ‚Äústandard constructions.‚Äù Despite their
independent origin, however, our question ‚Äúwhen does an endofunctor T arise
from an adjunction?‚Äù has the simple answer: just if it is the functor part of
a monad.

We have now given constructions back and forth between adjunctions and

In general, the answer is no. There may be many diÔ¨Äerent categories D and
adjunctions F ‚ä£U : D ‚ÜíC, all giving the same monad on C. We have used the
Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore category CT , but there is also something called the ‚ÄúKleisli
category,‚Äù which is in general diÔ¨Äerent from CT , but also has an adjoint pair to
C giving rise to the same monad (see the exercises).

Typical examples of monadic forgetful functors U : C ‚ÜíSets are those from

groups, rings, etc. Indeed, one can reasonably take monadicity as the deÔ¨Ånition
of being ‚Äúalgebraic.‚Äù
An example of a right adjoint that is not monadic is the forgetful functor
from posets,
U : Pos ‚ÜíSets.
Its left adjoint F is the discrete poset functor. For any set X, therefore, one has
as the unit the identity function X = UF(X). The reader can easily show that
the Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore category for T = 1Sets is then just Sets itself.

Œ¥ = FŒ∑U : G ‚ÜíG2.
The notions of coalgebra for a comonad, and of a comonadic functor, are
of course also precisely dual to the corresponding ones for monads. Why do
we even bother to study these notions separately, rather than just considering
their duals? As in other examples of duality, there are actually two distinct
reasons:
1. We may be interested in a particular category with special properties not
had by its dual. A comonad on SetsC is of course a monad on (SetsC)
op,
but as we now know, SetsC has many special properties that its dual does
not have (e.g., it is a topos!). So we can proÔ¨Åtably consider the notion of a
comonad on such a category.
A simple example of this kind is the comonad G = Œî ‚ó¶lim
‚Üê‚àíresulting
from composing the ‚Äúconstant functor‚Äù functor Œî : Sets ‚ÜíSetsC with
the ‚Äúlimit‚Äù functor lim
‚Üê‚àí: SetsC ‚ÜíSets. It can be shown in general that
the coalgebras for this comonad again form a topos. In fact, they are just the
constant functors Œî(S) for sets S, and the category Sets is thus comonadic
over SetsC.
2. It may happen that both structures‚Äîmonad and comonad‚Äîoccur together,
and interact. Taking the opposite category will not alter this situation! This
happens for instance when a system of three adjoint functors are composed:
L ‚ä£U ‚ä£R

This arises, for instance, in the foregoing example with R = lim
‚Üê‚àí, and

as ‚ÄúS5.‚Äù
A related example is given by the open and closed subsets of a topological
space: the topological interior operation on arbitrary subsets is a comonad
and closure is a monad. We leave the details as an exercise.

Some very basic kinds of algebraic structures have a more simple description
than as algebras for a monad, and this description generalizes to structures that
are not algebras for any monad, but still have some algebra-like properties.

Finally, we ask, what is the relationship between algebras for endofunctors and
algebras for monads? The following proposition, which is a sort of ‚Äúfolk theorem,‚Äù
gives the answer.
Proposition 10.14. Let the category S have Ô¨Ånite coproducts. Given an endo-
functor P : S ‚ÜíS, the following conditions are equivalent:
1. The P-algebras are the algebras for a monad. Precisely, there is a monad
(T : S ‚ÜíS, Œ∑, Œº), and an equivalence
P-Alg(S) ‚âÉST
between the category of P-algebras and the category ST of algebras for the
monad. Moreover, this equivalence preserves the respective forgetful functors
to S.
2. The forgetful functor U : P-Alg(S) ‚ÜíS has a left adjoint
F ‚ä¢U.
3. For each object A of S, the endofunctor
PA(X) = A + P(X) : S ‚ÜíS
has an initial algebra.
Proof. That (1) implies (2) is clear.

2. (a) Rel ‚àº= Relop. The isomorphism functor (in both directions) takes an
object A to itself, and takes a relation f ‚äÜA√óB to the opposite relation
f op ‚äÜB √ó A deÔ¨Åned by f op := {‚ü®b, a‚ü©| ‚ü®a, b‚ü©‚ààf}. It is straightforward
to check that this is a functor Rel ‚ÜíRelop and Relop ‚ÜíRel, and it is
its own inverse.
(b) Sets Ã∏‚àº= Setsop. Consider maps into the empty set ‚àÖ; there is exactly one.
If Sets ‚àº= Setsop held, there would have to be a corresponding set ‚àÖ‚Ä≤ with
exactly one arrow out of it.
(c) P(X) ‚àº= P(X)op. The isomorphism takes each element U of the powerset
to its complement X ‚àíU. Functoriality amounts to the fact that U ‚äÜV
implies X ‚àíV ‚äÜX ‚àíU.
3. (a) A bijection f from a set A to a set B, and its inverse f ‚àí1, comprise an
isomorphism; f(f ‚àí1(b)) = b and f ‚àí1(f(a)) = a, and so f ‚ó¶f ‚àí1 = 1B and
f ‚àí1 ‚ó¶f = 1A, by deÔ¨Ånition of the inverse. If an arrow f : A ‚ÜíB in Sets
is an isomorphism, then there is an arrow g : B ‚ÜíA such that f ‚ó¶g = 1B
and g ‚ó¶f = 1A. The arrow f is an injection because f(a) = f(a‚Ä≤) implies
a = g(f(a)) = g(f(a‚Ä≤)) = a‚Ä≤, and f is surjective because every b ‚ààB has
a preimage, namely g(b), since f(g(b)) = b.

11. (a) The functor M : Sets ‚ÜíMon that takes a set X to the free monoid on
X (i.e., strings over X and concatenation) and takes a function f : X ‚ÜíY
to the function M(f) deÔ¨Åned by M(f)(a1 . . . ak) = f(a1) . . . f(ak) is a func-
tor; M(f) is a monoid homomorphism MX ‚ÜíMY since it preserves the
monoid identity (the empty string) and the monoid operation (composi-
tion). It can be checked that M preserves identity functions and composition:
M(1X)(a1 . . . ak) = 1X(a1) . . . 1X(ak) = a1 . . . ak and
M(g ‚ó¶f)(a1 . . . ak) = (g ‚ó¶f)(a1) . . . (g ‚ó¶f)(ak)
= g(f(a1)) . . . g(f(ak)) = M(g)(M(f)(a1 . . . ak))
= (M(g) ‚ó¶M(f))(a1 . . . ak).
12. Let D be a category and h : G ‚ÜíU(D) be a graph homomorphism. Suppose
¬Øh is a functor C(G) ‚ÜíD such that
U(¬Øh) ‚ó¶i = h
(‚àó).
From this equation, we see that U(¬Øh)(i(x)) = h(x) for all vertices and
edges x ‚ààG. So the behavior of ¬Øh on objects and paths of length one
(i.e., arrows in the image of i) in C(G) is completely determined by the
requirement (‚àó). But since ¬Øh is assumed to be a functor, and so must pre-
serve composition, its behavior on arrows in C(G) that correspond to longer
paths in G is also determined, by a simple induction. Now it must be that
¬Øh(f1 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ fk) = h(f1) ‚ó¶¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ h(fk) if ¬Øh is a functor, and similarly ¬Øh(ŒµA) = 1A,
where ŒµA is the empty path at A. So uniqueness of ¬Øh is established, and
it is easily checked that this deÔ¨Ånition is indeed a functor, so the UMP is
satisÔ¨Åed.

1. Suppose f : A ‚ÜíB is epi and not surjective. Choose b ‚ààB not in the range
of f. DeÔ¨Åne g1, g2 : B ‚Üí{0, 1} as follows: g1(x) = 0 for all x ‚ààB, and

of b, a contradiction. In the other direction, suppose f is surjective, and
suppose g1, g2 : B ‚ÜíC are such that g1 Ã∏= g2. Then there is b ‚ààB such
that g1(b) Ã∏= g2(b). By assumption, b has a preimage a such that f(a) = b.
So g1(f(a)) Ã∏= g2(f(a)) and g1 ‚ó¶f Ã∏= g2 ‚ó¶f.
4. (a) Iso: the inverse of h is f ‚àí1 ‚ó¶g‚àí1. Monic: If h‚ó¶k1 = h‚ó¶k2, then g‚ó¶f‚ó¶k1 =
g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k2. Since g is monic, f ‚ó¶k1 = f ‚ó¶k2. Since f is monic, k1 = k2. Epi:
dual argument.
(b) If f ‚ó¶k1 = f ‚ó¶k2, then g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k1 = g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k2. Since h is monic, k1 = k2.
(c) Dual argument to (b).
(d) In Sets, put A = C = {0}, B = {0, 1}, and all arrows constantly 0. h is
monic but g is not.

5. Suppose f : A ‚ÜíB is an isomorphism. Then f is mono because f ‚ó¶k1 = f ‚ó¶k2
implies k1 = f ‚àí1 ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k1 = f ‚àí1 ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k2 = k2, and dually f is mono also.
Trivially, f is split mono and split epi because f ‚ó¶f ‚àí1 = 1B and f ‚àí1‚ó¶f = 1A.
So we know (a)‚áí(b), (c), (d). If f is mono and split epi, then there is g such
that f‚ó¶g = 1B. But since f is mono, (f‚ó¶g)‚ó¶f = f‚ó¶(g‚ó¶f) = f = f‚ó¶1A implies
g ‚ó¶f = 1A and so g is in fact the inverse of f, and we have (b)‚áí(a). Dually,
(c)‚áí(a). The fact that (d)‚áí(b), (c) needs only that split mono implies mono
(or dually that split epi implies epi). If there is g such that g ‚ó¶f = 1A, then
f ‚ó¶k1 = f ‚ó¶k2 implies k1 = g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k1 = g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶k2 = k2.
6. If h : G ‚ÜíH is injective on edges and vertices, and h ‚ó¶f = h ‚ó¶g in Graphs,
then the underlying set functions on edges and vertices are mono arrows in
Sets, so the edge and vertex parts of f and g are equal, and so f = g. If
h : G ‚ÜíH is mono in Graphs, and it is not injective on vertices, then
there are two vertices v, w such that h(v) = h(w). Let 1 be the graph with
one vertex, and f, g be graph homomorphisms 1 ‚ÜíG taking that vertex
to v, w, respectively. Then, h ‚ó¶f = h ‚ó¶g. A similar argument holds for
edges.
9. First, in the category Pos, an arrow is epi iÔ¨Äit is surjective: suppose that
f : A ‚ÜíB is surjective and let g, h : B ‚ÜíC with gf = hf. In Pos, this
means that g and h agree on the image of f, which by surjectivity is all of B.
Hence g = h and f is epi. On the other hand, suppose f is not epi and that
g, h : B ‚ÜíC witness this. Since g Ã∏= h, there is some b ‚ààB with g(b) Ã∏= h(b).
But from this b Ã∏‚ààf(A), and so A is not surjective.
Next, the singleton set 1, regarded as a poset, is projective: suppose f : 1 ‚Üí
Y and e : X ‚Ü†Y are arrows in Pos, with e epi. Then e is surjective, so there
is some x ‚ààX with e(x) = f(‚àó). Any map ‚àó‚Üíx witnesses the projectivity
of 1.
10. Any set A is projective in Pos: suppose that f : A ‚ÜíY and e : X ‚Ü†Y are
arrows in Pos. Choose for each y ‚ààY an element xy ‚ààX with f(xy) = y; this
is possible since e is epi and hence surjective. Now deÔ¨Åne a map ¬Øf : A ‚ÜíX
by a ‚Üíxf(a). Since A is discrete this is necessarily monotonic, and we have
e ¬Øf = f, so A is projective.
For contrast, the two element poset P = {0 ‚â§1} is not projective. Indeed,
we may take f to be the identity and X to be the discrete two-element set
{a, b}. Then the surjective map e : a ‚Üí0, b ‚Üí1 is an epi, since it is surjective.
However, any monotone map g : P ‚Üí{a, b} must identify 0 and 1, since the
only arrows in the second category are identities. But then e ‚ó¶g Ã∏= 1P . Thus,
there is no function g lifting the identity map on P across e, so P is not
projective.
Moreover, every projective object in Pos is discrete: For suppose Q is projec-
tive. We can always consider the discretation |Q| of Q, which has the same
objects as Q and only identity arrows. We clearly get a map |Q| ‚ÜíQ which

of maps and identity arrows of discrete posets are exactly those of Set, so
Set is a subcategory of Pos. Moreover, every function between discrete sets
is monotone, so this is a full subcategory.

13. Let P be the iterated product A√ó(B√óC) with the obvious maps p1 : P ‚ÜíA,
p2 : P ‚ÜíB√óC ‚ÜíB, and p3 : P ‚ÜíB√óC ‚ÜíC. DeÔ¨Åne Q = (A√óB)√óC and

qif = pi. We can run a similar argument to get a map g in the other direction.
Composing, we get gf : P ‚ÜíP which respects the pi. By the UMP, such a
map is unique, but the identity is another such map. Thus they must be the
same, so gf = 1P . Similarly fg = 1Q, so f and g are inverse and P ‚àº= Q.

1. Given a categorical congruence ‚àºon a group G, the corresponding normal
subgroup is N‚àº:= {g | g ‚àºe}. N is a subgroup; it contains the identity
by reÔ¨Çexivity of ‚àº. It is closed under inverse by symmetry and the fact that
e ‚àºg implies g‚àí1 = g‚àí1e ‚àºg‚àí1g = e. It is closed under product because if
g ‚àºe and h ‚àºe then gh ‚àºge = g ‚àºe, and by transitivity gh ‚ààN‚àº. It is
normal because

1. Their UMPs coincide. A product in C/X of f and g is an object h : A √óX
B ‚ÜíX and projections œÄ1 : h ‚Üíf and œÄ2 : h ‚Üíg which is terminal
among such structures. The pullback of f, g requires an object A √óX B and
projections œÄ1 : A √óX B ‚ÜíA and œÄ2 : A √óX B ‚ÜíB such that f ‚ó¶œÄ1 =
g ‚ó¶œÄ2, terminal among such structures. The commutativity requirements of
the pullback are exactly those imposed by the deÔ¨Ånition of arrow in the slice
category.

12. If we let the numeral n denote the initial segment of the natural number

0 ‚Üí1 ‚Üí2 ‚Üí. . . ‚Üín ‚Üí. . . .
We would like to determine the limit and colimit of the diagram.
For the limit, suppose we have a cone Œ∂n : Z ‚Üín. Since 0 is the initial object,
Œ∂0 is constant, and each map Œ∂n has Œ∂0 as a factor (this is the cone condition).
But each such map simply takes 0 to itself, regarded as an element of n, so
that Œ∂n is also the constant zero map. So the limit of the diagram can be
(anything isomorphic to) the object 0 together with the inclusions 0 ‚Üín.
Now suppose we have a co-cone œàn : n ‚ÜíY . The co-cone condition implies
that œàn is simply the restriction of œàm+n to the subset n ‚äÜn + m. If m < n,

6. Here we consider the category Sub, whose objects are pairs (A, P ‚äÜA), and
whose arrows f : (A, P) ‚Üí(B, Q) are set functions A ‚ÜíB such that a ‚ààP
iÔ¨Äf(a) ‚ààQ. This means that an arrow in this category is essentially a pair

preserves cartesian closure, so Sub is also cartesian closed.

4. Both functors are faithful. U is full because every monoid homomorphism
between groups is a group homomorphism: if h(ab) = h(a)h(b) then e =
h(a‚àí1a) = h(a‚àí1)h(a) and symmetrically e = h(a)h(a‚àí1) and so h(a‚àí1)
is the inverse of h(a). V is not full; there are set functions between mono-
ids that are not homomorphisms. Only V is surjective on objects (there

294
SOLUTIONS TO SELECTED EXERCISES
are, for example cyclic groups of every cardinality). Only U is injective
on objects, since monoid structure uniquely determines inverses, if they
exist.
5. It is easy to check that upward-closed sets are closed under unions and Ô¨Ånite
intersections. The arrow part of the functor A simply takes a monotone
function f : P ‚ÜíQ to itself, construed as a function f : A(P) ‚ÜíA(Q).
Preservation of identities and composition is therefore trivial, but we must
check that f is in fact an arrow in Top. Let U be an open (that is, upward-
closed) subset of A(Q). We must show that f ‚àí1(U) is upward-closed. Let
x ‚ààf ‚àí1(U) and y ‚ààP be given, and suppose x ‚â§y. We know that f(x) ‚ààU
and f(x) ‚â§f(y) since f is monotone. Because U is upward-closed, we have
f(y) ‚ààU, so y ‚ààf ‚àí1(U) and so f is continuous.
A is trivially faithful. A is also full: Let f be a continuous function P ‚ÜíQ.
Put D := {q ‚ààQ | f(x) ‚â§q}. Since f is continuous and D is upward-closed,
f ‚àí1(D) is upward-closed. If x ‚â§y then the fact that x ‚ààf ‚àí1(D) implies
y ‚ààf ‚àí1(D) and so f(y) ‚ààD. That is, f(x) ‚â§f(y). Hence every continuous
function A(P) ‚ÜíA(Q) is a monotone function P ‚ÜíQ.

also one. Conversely, if p : FC ‚áÑFC‚Ä≤ : q is an iso, then since F is full
there are f : C ‚áÑC‚Ä≤ : g with Ff = p and Fg = q. Then g ‚ó¶f = 1C
since F(g ‚ó¶f) = Fg ‚ó¶Ff = 1F C = F(1C), and F is faithful. Similarly,
f ‚ó¶g = 1C‚Ä≤.

3. The following isos are natural in Z:
homC(Z, AB √ó AC) ‚àº= homC(Z, AB) √ó homC(Z, AC)
‚àº= homC(Z √ó B, A) √ó homC(Z √ó C, A)
‚àº= homC((Z √ó B) + (Z √ó C), A)
‚àº= homC(Z √ó (B + C), A)
‚àº= homC(Z, AB+C).
Hence AB √ó AC ‚àº= AB+C, since the Yoneda embedding is full and faithful.
The case of (A √ó B)C ‚àº= AC √ó BC is similar.

10. A right adjoint to U : C/C ‚ÜíC is given by products with C,
A ‚Üí(œÄ2 : A √ó C ‚ÜíC),

terminal, in which case C/C ‚àº= C.

2. Let C be a category with terminal object 1 and binary coproducts, and deÔ¨Åne
T : C ‚ÜíC by TC = 1+C. Let T be the equational theory of a set equipped
with a unary operation and a distinguished constant (no equations). We want
to show that the following categories are equivalent:
T-algebras
Objects :
(A ‚ààC, a : 1 + A ‚ÜíA)
Arrows :
h : (A, a) ‚Üí(B, b) s.t. h ‚ó¶a = b ‚ó¶T(h)
T-algebras
Objects :
(X ‚ààSets, cX ‚ààX, sX : X ‚ÜíX)
Arrows :
f : X ‚ÜíY s.t fcX = cY and f ‚ó¶sX = sY ‚ó¶f
We have the functor F : T-Alg ‚ÜíT-Alg sending
(A, a) ‚Üí(A, a1 : 1 ‚ÜíA, a2 : A ‚ÜíA)
where a = [a1, a2] as a map from the coproduct 1 + A.
Conversely, given (X, c ‚ààX, s : X ‚ÜíX), we can set f = [c, s] : 1 + X ‚ÜíX
to get a T-algebra. The eÔ¨Äect on morphisms is easily seen, as is the fact that
these are pseudo-inverse functors.

Since free T-algebras exist in Sets and such existence is preserved by equi-
valence functors, it follows that Sets has free T-algebras. In particular, an
initial T-algebra in Sets is the initial T-algebra N, which is an NNO.
